## Transcript

- [00:00] Clive Wearing was playing the piano alone in his room.
- [00:04] When his wife came into the room, he immediately leapt up and embraced her with joyful enthusiasm.
- [00:09] A minute later, she slipped out to grab a glass of water, and when she returned, he
- [00:13] gave her that same bright greeting, as if shed been gone for days.
- [00:17] And then he did again.
- [00:19] And again.
- [00:20] Clive was an accomplished London musician, until, in 1985 at the age 47, he contracted
- [00:25] a rare Herpes encephalitis virus that ravaged his central nervous system.
- [00:30] Since then hes been unable to remember almost any of his past, or to make any new
- [00:36] memories.
- [00:37] His wife is the only person he recognizes, but he can never recall the last time he saw
- [00:40] her.
- [00:41] This may be the most profound case of extreme and chronic amnesia ever recorded.
- [00:46] Our memory helps make us who we are.
- [00:48] Whether recognizing loved ones, recalling past joys, or just remembering how to, like,
- [00:53] walk and talk and fry an egg, memory is the chain that connects our past to our present.
- [00:58] If it breaks, were left untethered, incapable of leaving the present moment, and unable
- [01:04] to embrace the future.
- [01:05] But memory isnt an all or nothing thing, of course.
- [01:09] Wearing cant remember any details about his personal past, but he still remembers
- [01:12] how to speak English, get dressed, and play the piano.
- [01:15] Some memories you process automatically, and they are stored differently than your more
- [01:19] personal or factual memories, like, your first kiss, or how to recite pi to twelve places,
- [01:23] or who won the Peloponnesian War.
- [01:25] Speaking of ancient Greeks -- and to help demonstrate what Im talking about -- I
- [01:28] want you to have a look at our Spartan friend here, and remember his name.
- [01:32] Cause were gonna test your memory in just minute.
- [01:38] [INTRO]
- [01:42] Technically, memory is learning that has persisted over time -- information that has been stored
- [01:50] and, in many cases, can be recalled.
- [01:52] Except of course during the exam!
- [01:54] Our memories are typically accessed in three different ways  through recall, recognition,
- [01:59] and relearning.
- [02:00] And if you think about all the different kinds of tests youve taken in school, theyre
- [02:04] all actually designed to size up how you access stored information in these ways.
- [02:08] Like, recall is how you reach back in your mind and bring up information, just as you
- [02:13] do in fill-in-the-blank tests.
- [02:14] So if i say, BLANK is the capital of Greece, your brain would hopefully recall the answer
- [02:19] as Athens.
- [02:20] Recognition, meanwhile, is more like a multiple-choice test -- you only need identify old information
- [02:25] when presented with it.
- [02:27] As in: which of the following was NOT an ancient city in Greece: Athens, Marathon, Pompeii,
- [02:33] or Sparta.
- [02:34] And relearning is sort of like refreshing or reinforcing old information.
- [02:38] So when you study for a final exam, you relearn things you half-forgot more easily than you
- [02:43] did when you were first learning them, like, say, a basic timeline of the Greek empire.
- [02:48] But how?
- [02:49] How does all of that data that were exposed to, all the time, every day, become memory?
- [02:54] Well, in the late 1960s, American psychologists Richard Atkinson and Richard Shiffrin figured
- [02:59] out enough about the process of memory-formation to break it down into three stages:
- [03:03] First, its first encoded into brain, then stored for future use, and then eventually
- [03:07] retrieved.
- [03:09] Sounds simple, but by now youve figured out that, just because you take a lot of stuff
- [03:12] about your mind for granted, that doesnt mean it isnt complicated.
- [03:15] By Atkinson and Shiffrins model, we first record things we want to remember as an immediate,
- [03:20] but fleeting, sensory memory.
- [03:23] Think back to the image I showed you a minute ago.
- [03:24] Do you remember his name?
- [03:25] If you do its because you successfully managed to shuffle it into your short-term
- [03:30] memory, where you probably encoded it through rehearsal.
- [03:33] This is how you briefly remember something like a password or phone number-- hey Tommy,
- [03:37] whats Jennys number?
- [03:38] Okay.
- [03:39] 867-5309...867-5309 8-6-7-5-3 see youre getting it in your head there.
- [03:47] Or in this case, I told you to remember that guys name, so maybe you were thinking Leonidas
- [03:52] repeatedly over and over, even if you didnt think you were doing it.
- [03:54] But this information really only stays in your short-term memory for under thirty seconds
- [03:58] without a lot of rehearsal.
- [04:00] So if you werent repeating Leonidas, youd probably have
- [04:03] forgotten it already.
- [04:04] Because your mind, amazing as it is, can really only hold between four to seven distinct bits
- [04:09] of information at a time--at which point, the memory either decays, or gets transferred
- [04:14] into long-term memory.
- [04:16] Long-term memory is like your brains durable and ridiculously spacious storage unit, holding
- [04:21] all your knowledge, skills, and experiences.
- [04:24] Now, since the days of Atkinson and Shiffrin, psychologists have recognized that the classical
- [04:28] definition of short-term memory didnt really capture all of the processes involved in the
- [04:33] transfer of information to your long-term memory.
- [04:35] I mean, its more than just being able to remember some Greek guys name.
- [04:38] So later generations of psychologists revisited the whole idea of short-term memory and updated
- [04:43] it to the more comprehensive concept of working memory.
- [04:46] Working memory involves all the ways that we take short-term information and stash it
- [04:51] in our long-term stores.
- [04:52] And increasingly, we think of it as involving both explicit and implicit processes.
- [04:56] When we store information consciously and actively, thats an explicit process.
- [05:01] We make the most of this aspect of working memory when we study, for instance, so that
- [05:05] we can know that Athens is the capital of Greece, and that Pompeii was a Roman town,
- [05:08] and not a Greek one.
- [05:09] This is how we capture facts and knowledge that we think were going to need -- like
- [05:13] I told you specifically to remember Leonidas name, you concentrated on that detail and
- [05:18] filed it away, if briefly.
- [05:20] But of course were not conscious of Every.
- [05:21] Tiny.
- [05:22] Thing that we take in.
- [05:23] Yet, our working memory often transfers stuff were not aware of to long-term storage.
- [05:29] We call that an implicit process, the kind you dont have to actively concentrate on.
- [05:33] A good example might be classically conditioned associations, like, if you get all sweaty
- [05:37] and nervous at the dentist because you had a root canal last year.
- [05:39] You dont need to pull up that file on the last time you got your face drilled to think
- [05:44] oh hey, oral surgery!
- [05:45] Not my favorite!
- [05:47] Instead, implicit processes cover all that stuff automatically.
- [05:49] This kind of automatic processing is hard to shut off-- unless youve got something
- [05:53] unusual going on in your brain, you may not have much choice but to learn this way, like
- [05:58] how you learned how to not put your hand into a fire.
- [06:01] That learning would have happened pretty much automatically as soon as you first yanked
- [06:04] your hand away from an open flame.
- [06:05] Whether things are lodged in there explicitly or implicitly, or both, there are also different
- [06:10] kinds of long-term memory.
- [06:12] For instance, procedural memory refers to how we remember to do things -- like riding
- [06:16] a bike or reading - its effortful to learn at first, but eventually you can do it without
- [06:21] thinking about it.
- [06:22] Long-term memory can also be episodic, tied to specific episodes of your life -- like
- [06:27] remember that time that Bernice fell out of her chair in chemistry lab and started
- [06:30] laughing uncontrollably?
- [06:31] Man, good times.
- [06:32] There are other types of long-term memory, too, and were continually learning more
- [06:36] about the biology and psychology of the whole complex phenomenon.
- [06:40] For instance, while Clive Wearings episodic memories (among others) seem to be deeply
- [06:45] affected, his procedural memories for things seems to be in one piece.
- [06:49] This has to do with neuroanatomy that we dont have time to explore here, and that we dont
- [06:53] yet fully understand -- Wearing and others have a lot to teach us about the different
- [06:57] types of long-term memory storage.
- [06:59] Now, for healthy memories, there are lots of little tricks you can use to help remember
- [07:02] information.
- [07:03] Mnemonics, for one, help with memorization, and Im sure you know a few that take the
- [07:07] form of acronyms--ROY G. BIV for the colors of the rainbow, for instance.
- [07:11] Mnemonics work in part by organizing items into familiar, manageable units, in a process
- [07:16] called chunking.
- [07:17] For example, it may be hard to recall a seven-digit number, but itll be easier to commit it
- [07:22] to memory in the rhythm of a phone number: 867-5309.
- [07:24] Or you could just, you know, write a song about it.
- [07:28] Strategies like mnemonics and chunking can help you with explicit processing, but how
- [07:32] well you retain your data can depend on how deep you dig through the different levels
- [07:37] of processing.
- [07:38] Shallow processing, for instance, lets you encode information on really basic auditory
- [07:42] or visual levels, based on the sound, structure or appearance of a word.
- [07:46] So if youre trying to commit the name Leonidas to your explicit memory, using shallow processing
- [07:51] you might encode the word by recalling the cool font you saw it in.
- [07:54] But to really retain that information, youd want to activate your deep processing, which
- [07:59] encodes semantically, based on actual meaning associated with the word.
- [08:04] In this case, you might remember the story of the mega-tough yet very scantily clad warrior
- [08:09] of ancient Greece.
- [08:10] Or you might remember that leon means lion in Greek, that lions are tough
- [08:14] fighters, and that Leonidas was a tough Spartan warrior-king.
- [08:18] And then to really, really make it stick, you want to connect it to something meaningful
- [08:21] or related to your own personal, emotional experience.
- [08:24] Like maybe Gerard Butlers bronzed eight-pack torso and unconquerable blood-lust helped
- [08:28] lock down the words Spartan and Leonidas in your forever memory.
- [08:32] I mean, maybe.
- [08:33] If  if that helps you.
- [08:34] In the end, how much information you encode and remember depends on both the time you
- [08:38] took to learn it and how you made it personally relevant to YOU.
- [08:42] Memory is extremely powerful.
- [08:44] Its constantly shaping and reshaping your brain, your life, and your identity.
- [08:49] Clive Wearing is still himself on the outside, but in his inability to recall who he was,
- [08:53] or process what has happened, he has lost some critically important part of himself.
- [08:59] Our memories may haunt us or sustain us, but either way, they define us.
- [09:03] Without them, we are left to wander alone in the dark.
- [09:07] If you were committing this lesson to memory, you learned about how we encode and store
- [09:11] memory, the difference between implicit automatic and effortful explicit processing, how shallow
- [09:16] and deep processing work, and a few types of long-term storage.
